        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '31bc9933-b901-4f18-9154-a9473623c654'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d261297433" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>The modified NBTP-0.01 anode has the highest cycle life and best rate performance in this work. Furthermore, the expansion of the charge cut-off voltage resulted from the inhibition of the hydrogen evolution reaction by the Bi doping can greatly increase the discharge capacity and energy density of the modified NBTP-0.01//NMO full cell in contrast with those of the pristine NTP//NMO one, respectively.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1662f7b2-6034-4e4f-a9c3-8a1f1a517a65/batt202400767-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1662f7b2-6034-4e4f-a9c3-8a1f1a517a65/batt202400767-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ba01b97d-48a1-41b9-83a1-3be23cc53618/batt202400767-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1662f7b2-6034-4e4f-a9c3-8a1f1a517a65/batt202400767-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d261297528" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Aqueous sodium-ion batteries (ASIBs) show enormous difficulty to develop appropriate anode materials for their commercialization, mainly owing to the easy occurrence of hydrogen evolution from the decomposition of water at a negative potential with overlapping the operating potential of the anode for the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/extraction reaction. Here, new anode materials Na<sub>1+x</sub>Bi<sub>x</sub>Ti<sub>2−x</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C (x=0, 0.005, 0.01, 0.02) composites are prepared through introducing Bi elements into NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NTP, a promising anode material for ASIBs) with a facile sol-gel method. Thus, it firstly reports that the Bi doping can contribute to the inhibition of hydrogen evolution via regulation the electrode potential for hydrogen evolution at the anode, also accompanying with excellent electrochemical and charge/discharge performances. The full cell constructed by Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> as cathode and Bi-modified NTP as anode, exhibits the better cycling performance with a larger capacity retention of about 80.1 % at 2 C-rate for 800 cycles and 80.2 % at 10 C-rate for 1400 cycles in contrast with the pristine one (71.2 %, 56.2 %), respectively. Furthermore, the expansion of the charge cut-off voltage resulted from the inhibition of hydrogen evolution after Bi doping into NTP can finally increase the discharge capacity and energy density of the modified full cell by 21.8 % and 17.3 %, respectively.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="batt202400767-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="batt202400767-sec-0001-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In recent years, with the rapid development of modern portable electronic products, new energy vehicles, and grid energy storage technology, the demand for advanced battery energy storage systems has greatly increased.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0001_R_d261297400e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">1</a>-<a href="#batt202400767-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0003_R_d261297400e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Power Sources">3</a></sup></span> Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have gained widespread attention due to their abundant reserves and low cost, and show enormous potential in large-scale renewable energy storage.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0004_R_d261297400e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Energy Storage">4</a>-<a href="#batt202400767-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0008_R_d261297400e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Carbon Energy">8</a></sup></span> Therefore, the development of high energy density, stable, and sustainable sodium ion battery materials still requires a lot of research work.</p>
         
         <p>Organic SIBs utilize toxic, flammable, and volatile organic electrolytes, facing environmental challenges and safety risks.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0009_R_d261297400e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nano Energy">9</a>, <a href="#batt202400767-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0010_R_d261297400e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Small Methods">10</a></sup></span> Aqueous batteries use water-based electrolytes, which have advantages such as cost-effectiveness, safety, high power density, and scalable energy storage.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0011_R_d261297400e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energies">11</a>-<a href="#batt202400767-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0013_R_d261297400e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Rev.">13</a></sup></span> In the field of commercial aqueous batteries, the main challenges include limited electrochemical stability window of water, limited charge storage capacity and poor cycling stability of electrode materials.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0014_R_d261297400e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano-Micro Lett.">14</a>, <a href="#batt202400767-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0015_R_d261297400e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Storage Mater.">15</a></sup></span> Therefore, there is an urgent need to promote the development of electrochemical active materials with high discharge capacity and long cycle life in aqueous electrolytes, as this is crucial for the successful commercialization of aqueous sodium ion batteries (ASIBs). Different electrode materials for SIBs have been explored in recent years, including transition metal layered oxides,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0001_R_d261297400e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">1</a></sup></span> Prussian blue analogues,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0016_R_d261297400e532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Mater. Today Energy">16</a></sup></span> and polyanionic compounds.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0017_R_d261297400e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#batt202400767-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0018_R_d261297400e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbon Energy">18</a></sup></span> Among them, phosphate-based polyanionic compounds are fairly optimal as negative electrode materials due to their stability, safety, low cost, and structural diversity.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0019_R_d261297400e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Sci.">19</a></sup></span> Due to its structural stability and diversity, phosphate framework materials are not only typical solid ion conductors,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0020_R_d261297400e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chem. Rev.">20</a></sup></span> but also low-cost electrode materials that are very suitable for other devices such as sodium ion batteries and lithium ion batteries.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0021_R_d261297400e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">21</a>, <a href="#batt202400767-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0022_R_d261297400e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Among these phosphate-based compounds, the sodium (Na) superionic conductor (NASICON)-type structure is considered to provide a highly conductive pathway for Na<sup>+</sup> migration, thus possessing strong sodium storage performance.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0023_R_d261297400e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a></sup></span> Among the NASICON-type compounds, sodium titanium phosphate (NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, NTP) has received increasing attention due to its high theoretical capacity of 133 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, low cost, good safety, high structural stability, open channels, and flat charge/discharge plateaus.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0024_R_d261297400e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">24</a>-<a href="#batt202400767-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0028_R_d261297400e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span> It is recognized as one of the most promising negative electrode materials in ASIBs. However, the NASICON structure also places restrictions on the electrical conductivity of NTP.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0029_R_d261297400e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Appl. Electrochem.">29</a></sup></span> Moreover, some studies have reported that the NTP material commonly tends to lose function over tens or several hundred cycles, especially when cycled deeply or slowly at low C-rates less than 1 C, leading to the inferior cycling stability of the NTP anode.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0030_R_d261297400e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Electrochim. Acta">30</a>, <a href="#batt202400767-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0031_R_d261297400e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">31</a></sup></span> The poor cycling stability of NTP is mainly attributed by the possible dissolution of NTP active materials in aqueous electrolytes and the hydrogen evolution from the decomposition of water.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0032_R_d261297400e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Mater. Futures">32</a>, <a href="#batt202400767-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0033_R_d261297400e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Chem. Commun.">33</a></sup></span> Thus, some effective modifications of NTP including ion doping<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0029_R_d261297400e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Appl. Electrochem.">29</a>, <a href="#batt202400767-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0034_R_d261297400e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Ceram. Int.">34</a></sup></span> and coating NTP with conductive materials<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0035_R_d261297400e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Electrochemistry">35</a>-<a href="#batt202400767-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0038_R_d261297400e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Solid State Ionics">38</a></sup></span> have been proposed to promote the electrical conductivity and cycling stability of NTP.</p>
         
         <p>On one hand, the doping of impurity atoms into NTP, especially partially or fully replacing Ti element in NTP with elements such as Mn,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0039_R_d261297400e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Chem. Commun.">39</a></sup></span> Mg,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0040_R_d261297400e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Chemi. – Eur. J.">40</a></sup></span> V,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0041_R_d261297400e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Nano Res.">41</a></sup></span> Fe,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0042_R_d261297400e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Mater. Chem. A">42</a>, <a href="#batt202400767-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0043_R_d261297400e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nano Energy">43</a></sup></span> can improve its ionic conductivity, structural stability, and electrochemical performance to some extent, thereby promoting its discharge capacity and cycling stability. The authors also prepared NASICON-structured Na<sub>1.1</sub>Al<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>1.9</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0029_R_d261297400e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Appl. Electrochem.">29</a></sup></span> and Na<sub>3.4</sub>Mn<sub>1.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.8</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0044_R_d261297400e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Alloys Compd.">44</a></sup></span> composites, applied as the promising anode and cathode materials in ASIBs, respectively. On the other hand, coating NTP with conductive carbon materials,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0035_R_d261297400e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Electrochemistry">35</a>, <a href="#batt202400767-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0036_R_d261297400e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">36</a></sup></span> polypyrrole (PPY) conductive polymers<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0038_R_d261297400e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Solid State Ionics">38</a></sup></span> and TiN<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0037_R_d261297400e633" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chem. Eng. J.">37</a></sup></span> have been recently obtained to hinder the direct contact between the NTP anode and the aqueous electrolyte, and thus suppress the possible dissolution of NTP active materials in aqueous electrolytes, accompanying with the improvement of the electrical conductivity and cycling stability of NTP. An artificial poly-pyrrole protective interphase layer was also constructed by the authors for the NTP anode to suppress the unwanted side reactions at the electrode/electrolyte interface, and obtained some desired results.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0038_R_d261297400e636" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Solid State Ionics">38</a></sup></span> Although a variety of methods have been used to promote the electrical conductivity and cycling stability of NTP, it should still be noted that the key obstacle that prevents the popularization of NTP in ASIBs is the inadequate operating potential (−0.581 V vs. NHE) for the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/extraction reaction, which is too close to the hydrogen evolution potential (−0.591 V vs. NHE).<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0042_R_d261297400e641" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Mater. Chem. A">42</a></sup></span> Then, during the NTP charging process, a potential overlap between sodiation and H<sub>2</sub>O reductive decomposition might come out easily, accompanying with the occurrence of hydrogen evolution at the NTP anode, even if the electrode polarization potentials contributed by the kinetic factors in the reaction process are considered. Furthermore, the above-mentioned irreversible hydrogen evolution reaction might increase the internal pressure of the battery and deplete the limited Na<sup>+</sup> in the cathode material, thus decreasing the battery safety and capacity with a rapid deterioration.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0042_R_d261297400e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Mater. Chem. A">42</a></sup></span> Therefore, increasing the operating potential of NTP and decreasing the hydrogen evolution potential are very important technical keys to suppress hydrogen evolution for commercializing NTP in ASIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Recently, Qiu et al<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0042_R_d261297400e656" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Mater. Chem. A">42</a></sup></span> have firstly reported a redox potential regulation strategy to overcome this technical problem by integrating the redox couples of Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> to yield Na<sub>1.5</sub>Ti<sub>1.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NTFP) and increasing its operating potential up to −0.498 V vs. NHE, which effectively prevents the potential overlap with the reductive decomposition of H<sub>2</sub>O. However, it is worth noting that increasing the operating potential of the NTP anode for the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/extraction process can inhibit the occurrence of hydrogen evolution, however, with the decreasing operating potential of the full cell during discharging, thereby reducing the discharge capacity and energy density of the full cell. Furthermore, if the inhibition of hydrogen evolution at the negative side was resulted from the decreasing potential for hydrogen evolution at the anode, rather than the increasing operating potential of NTP, the NTP anode could actually operate at a lower potential during the charge/discharge process. Thus, it could ensure that the full cell has a higher operating potential during discharge, thereby improving the discharge energy density, cycle life, and coulombic efficiency of the battery. Based on the above analysis, it is of great necessity to produce new anode materials for ASIBs to suppress the occurrence of hydrogen evolution via decreasing the potential for hydrogen evolution at the negative side.</p>
         
         <p>In this study, we prepared new anode materials Na<sub>1+x</sub>Bi<sub>x</sub>Ti<sub>2−x</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C (x=0, 0.005, 0.01, 0.02) composites through introducing Bi elements into NTP with a facile sol-gel method, and it firstly reports that the Bi doping can contribute to the inhibition of hydrogen evolution via regulation the electrode potential for hydrogen evolution at the NTP anode, also accompanying with excellent electrochemical and charge/discharge performances. Furthermore, the excellent stability and high efficiency of the aqueous half-cells and full-cells with long-term cycling suggests the importance of inhibiting electrolyte decomposition to maintain the Na<sup>+</sup> reversible activity in the full-cells.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="batt202400767-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="batt202400767-sec-0002-title"> Experimental</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400767-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202400767-sec-0003-title"> Materials Synthesis</h3>
            
            <p>The Na<sub>1+x</sub>Bi<sub>x</sub>Ti<sub>2−x</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C composites as anode were prepared by a facile sol-gel method, as reported in our previous work.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0029_R_d261297400e724" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Appl. Electrochem.">29</a>, <a href="#batt202400767-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0038_R_d261297400e724" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Solid State Ionics">38</a></sup></span> Firstly, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> and certain accounts of citric acid was mixed and solved in deionized water. And then, tetrabutyl titanate diluted by ethanol was added into the above-mentioned mixed solution with stirring on the electro-thermostatic water bath. After evaporation of the solvent, the precursor was sintered at 350 °C for 4 h and then 800 °C for 10 h under argon atmosphere to obtain the undoped NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C products. Then, the three Bi doped samples were synthesized in the same way, except that stoichiometric Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was added in the deionized water before the addition of tetrabutyl titanate diluted by ethanol. And the amount of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and tetrabutyl titanate should be adjusted based on the stoichiometric ratio of the target doped products. The obtained Na<sub>1+x</sub>Bi<sub>x</sub>Ti<sub>2−x</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C (x=0, 0.005, 0.01, 0.02) were denoted as NTP, NBTP-0.005, NBTP-0.01, and NBTP-0.02, respectively. It should be also noted that too much Bi doped with the value of x higher than 0.05, will easily result in the occurrence of certain impurities in the obtained samples except for the NTP/C main phase. Additionally, Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> as cathode was produced with MnCO<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> as starting materials by solid-state reaction, as similarly reported in the previous work,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0045_R_d261297400e774" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Electrochim. Acta">45</a></sup></span> and the corresponding process parameter optimization have been shown in detail in another unpublished paper submitted.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400767-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202400767-sec-0004-title"> Materials Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The crystal structure and phase component of the as-synthesized samples were identified by powder X-ray diffraction (XRD, Panlytical X'Pert-Pro) using the Kα radiation (λ=0.154 nm) in the range of 2<i>θ</i> from 10° to 90°. Raman spectroscopy (Raman, Horiba LabRam HR Evolution) was performed to confirm the presence of carbon materials in the obtained samples. The surface morphology of the samples was observed by using scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Zeiss Gemini 300) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEOL JEM-F200). X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo Fisher ESCALAB XI+) was performed to examine the elemental composition and valence state of samples, with XPS Peak 4.1 employed to deconvolve the Ti2p and Bi4 f peaks.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="batt202400767-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="batt202400767-sec-0005-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performances of the anodes in 1.0 M Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> aqueous electrolytes were examined with a conventional three-electrode cell using a platinum foil as the counter electrode and a saturated calomel electrode (SCE, 0.244 V) as the reference electrode. A salt bridge was applied to decrease the liquid junction potential between the Luggin capillary and the working electrode. The working electrode is composed of active material, carbon black, and polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE), with a mass ratio of 7 : 2 : 1. Briefly, active materials and carbon black were mixed under constant grinding in an agate mortar. Then PTFE was added to the mixture to form the electrode paste. The working electrode was obtained by pressing the paste onto a Ti mesh (100 mesh) current collector. The typical mass loading and thickness of the active material in the anode was approximately 6–7 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> and 50 μm, respectively. Cyclic voltammograms (CV) and potentiodynamic polarization curves were obtained with a Gamry 1000E Electrochemical Analysis Instrument. The galvanostatic charge/discharge measurements for the as-prepared anodes with the above-mentioned three-electrode cell were carried out on a Land CT2001A battery test system (Wuhan, China). The charge/discharge capacities were estimated in units of mAh g<sup>−1</sup> based on the mass of the NTP/C active materials of the anodes and the rates in the C-rate convention whereas 1 C corresponds to 133 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Unless otherwise indicated, all potentials reported in this work were referred to SCE.</p>
            
            <p>In addition, a CR2025 coin cell configuration was employed to examine the electrochemical performance of the full cell with NMO as cathode and Bi-doped NTP/C as anode. The full cell applied glass fiber as the separator and 1.0 M Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> as the aqueous electrolyte, respectively. The anode contains 6–7 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> active materials with thickness of 50 μ m, while the cathode has 12–13 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> NMO with thickness of 350 μm. Accordingly, the capacity ratio of the cathode and anode was about 1.2 : 1, which ensured an anode-limited full cell. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) and Galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were also conducted for the full cells. The specific capacity and specific energy density of the full cell with NMO as cathode and Bi-doped NTP/C as anode were also estimated based on the mass of active material in the anode.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="batt202400767-sec-0006">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="batt202400767-sec-0006-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The crystal structure and chemical composition of the as-synthesized samples in this work were identified by the X-ray diffraction analysis. As shown in Figure <a href="#batt202400767-fig-0001">1a</a>, all the diffraction peaks of the XRD patterns for the obtained products can be fully indexed based on the predicted JCPDS pattern of NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> with rhombohedral R-3c space group. It can be also observed that no impurity was detected in the patterns of the obtained samples, indicating that an appropriate proportion of Bi doping has litter affect the crystal structure of NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. In addition, there are no carbon dispersion peaks observed from the XRD patterns of Figure <a href="#batt202400767-fig-0001">1a</a>, suggesting that the carbon coating on the NTP surface exists in an amorphous form. It should be noted that the (113) diffraction peak gradually shifts to smaller angles with the increasing Bi doping content. It indicates that the unit cell volume has increased, possibly resulted from the larger ion radius of Bi<sup>3+</sup> (1.03 Å) than that of Ti<sup>4+</sup> (0.605 Å) introduced into the NTP crystal structure. Furthermore, the determined lattice cell parameters of the obtained phases in this work can be estimated by fitting with Jade 5.0, and then the cell volume can be also calculated, as illustrated in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#batt202400767-tbl-0001">1</a>. Thus, the change of the lattice parameters and cell volume with the Bi doping content for the above samples was presented in Figures <a href="#batt202400767-fig-0001">1b</a> and <a href="#batt202400767-fig-0001">c</a>. It can be seen that all the lattice parameters including the values of a, b and c almost increase when the value of x becomes larger, and the NTP unit cell volume increases from 1358.960 to 1361.497 Å<sup>3</sup>. It can be concluded that Bi<sup>3+</sup> is successfully doped into the NTP lattice. The increase in the unit cell volume indicates a larger transport channel for Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the NTP crystal structure, thus promoting the electrochemical kinetics. Also, it can be concluded that Bi<sup>3+</sup> is successfully doped into the NTP lattice. As reported in the previous work,<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0046_R_d261297400e870" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">46</a></sup></span> an ingenious strategy of Bi<sup>3+</sup>(1.03 Å) substitution at V<sup>3+</sup> (0.74 Å) site in Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) was also similarly proposed for developing the cathode in organic sodium ion batteries. The introduction of Bi into NVP can further expand the crystal structure inside the NVP, thus accelerating the migration of Na<sup>+</sup>, and the obtained structural framework of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2-x</sub>Bi<sub>x</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C also shows that the (Bi<sub>x</sub>V<sub>2-x</sub>O<sub>6</sub>) octahedra and (PO<sub>4</sub>) tetrahedra are co-topically attached to the C-axis, forming a larger gap of the polyanion unit for Na<sup>+</sup> migration.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0046_R_d261297400e910" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">46</a></sup></span> Owing to the similar ion radius and polyanion unit for the corresponding Ti<sup>4+</sup> and V<sup>3+</sup> ions, the obtained Na<sub>1+x</sub>Bi<sub>x</sub>Ti<sub>2−x</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C in this work might be thought to have a similar structural framework containing the (Bi<sub>x</sub>Ti<sub>2-x</sub>O<sub>6</sub>) octahedra and (PO<sub>4</sub>) tetrahedra with the above-mentioned Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2-x</sub>Bi<sub>x</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C, also forming a larger gap of the polyanion unit for Na<sup>+</sup> migration. Additionally, the carbon content of the above-mentioned composites can be estimated to be about 9 % through the thermogravimetric (TG) analysis, as reported in our previous work.<span><sup><a href="#batt202400767-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="batt202400767-bib-0038_R_d261297400e950" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Solid State Ionics">38</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="batt202400767-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/49b0bf4b-8164-44df-97db-52ad466429d5/batt202400767-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/49b0bf4b-8164-44df-97db-52ad466429d5/batt202400767-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/37f46b7e-3181-497c-9961-b1b9a1cbcc5c/batt202400767-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/49b0bf4b-8164-44df-97db-52ad466429d5/batt202400767-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

         </section>
